        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2591782058" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11655562805068875257"><b>Cathedral</b> is a highly regarded short story by the American writer and poet, Raymond Carver. It was first published in 1983. Here is the opening:<br/><br/><i>“This blind man, an old friend of my wife’s, he was on his way to spend the night. His wife had died. So he was visiting the dead wife’s relatives in Connecticut … She hadn’t seen him since she worked for him one summer in Seattle ten years ago. But she and the blind man had kept in touch. They made tapes and mailed them back and forth. I wasn’t enthusia</i></span>
  <span id="freeText11655562805068875257" style="display:none"><b>Cathedral</b> is a highly regarded short story by the American writer and poet, Raymond Carver. It was first published in 1983. Here is the opening:<br /><br /><i>“This blind man, an old friend of my wife’s, he was on his way to spend the night. His wife had died. So he was visiting the dead wife’s relatives in Connecticut … She hadn’t seen him since she worked for him one summer in Seattle ten years ago. But she and the blind man had kept in touch. They made tapes and mailed them back and forth. I wasn’t enthusiastic about his visit. He was no one I knew. And his being blind bothered me. My idea of blindness came from the movies. In the movies, the blind moved slowly and never laughed. Sometimes they were led by seeing eye dogs. Blind man in my house was not something I looked forward to.”</i><br /><br />Instantly we form an opinion of the narrator as a narrow-minded man, prejudiced through genuine ignorance. We also see another form of prejudice, when his wife tells the narrator that Robert and his wife, Beulah, were inseparable:<br /><br /><i>“She’d told me a little about the blind man’s wife. Her name was Beulah. Beulah! That’s a name for a coloured woman.<br />‘Was his wife a negro?’ I asked.”</i><br /><br />The narrator seems to be uneasy with this information, and he further denigrates the blind man in his mind, immediately assuming that he is superior to Robert in all ways, by thinking of how dreadful it must have been for Beulah not to have been seen by the man she loved:<br /><br /><i>“And then I found myself thinking what a pitiful life this woman must have led. Imagine a woman who could never see herself as she was seen in the eyes of her loved one. A woman who could go on day after day and never receive the smallest compliment from her beloved. A woman whose husband could never read the expression on her face, be it misery or something better … her last thought maybe this: that he never even knew what she looked like, and she on an express to the grave … Pathetic.”</i><br /><br />We have further internal condemnation when Robert arrives:<br /><br /><i>“This blind man, feature this, he was wearing a full beard! A beard on a blind man! Too much, I say.”</i><br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[As we proceed through the story, and the visit, we pick up that the narrator is an insecure man, perhaps a little isolated from the real world. This will be the first time his wife has seen Robert since her marriage, subsequent divorce, and remarriage. The narrator is clearly jealous of his wife’s strong connection and relationship with her friend, just as we suspect him to be jealous of his wife’s first husband. The narrator does not seem to be able to form such connections for himself. They all drink heavily and eat a large dinner, cube steak, scalloped potatoes, green beans, two slices of bread and butter, and strawberry pie. After dinner, they drink even more, and the narrator continues to watch Robert closely.<br /><br /><i>“From time to time, he’d turn his blind face towards me, put his hand under his beard, ask me something. How long had I been in my present position? (Three years.) Did I like my work? (I didn’t.) Was I going to stay with it? (What were the options?) Finally, when I thought he was beginning to run down, I got up and turned on the TV.”</i><br /><br />His wife, irritated, leaves the room to put on a robe, to the narrator’s dismay:<br /><br /><i>“I didn’t want to be left alone with a blind man. I asked him if he wanted another drink, and he said sure. Then I asked if he wanted to smoke some dope with me.”</i><br /><br />Robert says he’ll try it, and so he and the narrator share a joint of cannabis. His wife returns, but falls asleep, so the narrator is left with Robert and the television. To his consternation his wife’s robe falls open:<br /><br /><i>“exposing a juicy thigh. I reached to draw her robe back over her, and it was then I glanced at the blind man. What the hell! I flipped the robe open again.”</i><br /><br />He has decided not to bother. What does it matter - the man is blind! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Through his narrator, Raymond Carver describes the prejudices many sighted people have, if they do not come across blindness very often. So often I have seen my blind friends shunned through embarrassment, as some sighted people have an inability to look at a blind person. I have witnessed both astonishment and incomprehension, as well as embarrassment by some sighted people watching my friends eat. Perhaps most hurtful of all is an assumption that blind people will not care about the way they look. These are correct, and well observed common faults of the sighted, and all evidenced in this story.<br /><br />Perhaps the most accurate detail was the idea that the narrator’s wife and Robert became close through the exchange of cassette tapes. I am partially sighted myself, and spent years tapesponding (the correct term) before computers were so accessible. It was easy, frequent, and international, because most postal systems sent the cassettes free of charge. You certainly do get to know people very well indeed when you talk nonstop for three quarters of an hour or more. Plenty of marriages would not cope with such dedicated conversation, and in <b>Cathedral</b> we doubt whether the narrator could sustain this with his wife. Now of course, this dates the story, as compact cassette tapes are a rarity, and “old technology”. The story cannot be faulted, however, for being of its time. As I say, this was an authentic part.<br /><br />But other areas made me wince, such as the reference to “feeling people’s faces”. This is something which sighted people seem to conjure up, in the same spirit as “knowing” that blind people have exceptional hearing, to make up for their lack of vision. What? Like dogs? No. It is just an urban myth. To touch another person in that way assumes an intimacy, and such intimacy would be exactly the same whether you are blind or sighted. Why portray blind people as having inferior social skills? Yet we are given to understand that Robert asked the narrator’s wife if he could feel her face, when she had said goodbye all those years ago, and it was such a pivotal moment in her life, that she started to write poetry after it. Oh my!<br /><br />The story reveals all the aspects and problems; the connections and detachments between the three characters. There is a strong bond between the narrator’s wife and Robert, and we see that the narrator appears to long for a similar connection with his wife. He sits listening to his wife talk to Robert, and hopes to hear his name being mentioned, but it never is. He remains on the outside, isolated from their conversation.<br /><br />We know that Robert has a television of his own. Does this surprise you? The narrator is not sure what to make of this. Neither does he have a reaction when Robert says he can tell the difference between a black and white one and a colour one. Perhaps these would be common feelings for sighted people who know no visually impaired people, or perhaps it further demonstrates Robert’s confusion. Here in the UK, just the signal with no picture is free for all blind people. No license fee needs to be paid, but this is only suitable for those blind people who live alone or with other blind people. It was probable that Robert would like to have a colour television, because his wife had been sighted. Plus, since we assume colour televisions were relatively new for these characters, the colour one was probably better sound quality.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[As the two men become more affected by smoking weed, the narrator feels the need to attempt to describe what he sees on the television:<br /><br /><i>“I waited as long as I could. Then I felt I had to say something. I said, ‘They’re showing the outside of this cathedral now.”</i><br /><br />The narrator becomes more and more aware that he is unable to find the words to describe the cathedral in the documentary. From this part onwards I found it impossible to suspend my disbelief. Robert would already know what a cathedral looked like. He would have had plenty of descriptions, not to mention aids such as tactile pictures, and models. Visually impaired people can also experience these in public areas such as Art Galleries and so on. Next, Robert suggests that the narrator get a pencil and paper to draw it with. Really? I think he would be unlikely to draw. Some blind people do take photographs, to share and show their friends, but getting down on the floor and almost guiding the pen at the end, seemed beyond belief. It was totally to make the point about the ignorance of a sighted person, allied with a clumsy metaphor for being on the same level.<br /><br />The narrator does not want to open his eyes at the end. He seems unaware of his surroundings. The only other interpretation I can make of this ending, is that it is describing a drug induced “lightbulb moment”, when just for a moment, the narrator’s experience is artificially expanded to include all sorts of other possibilities. Either that, or it is a metaphor for a spiritual experience, since these two have already touched on whether the narrator is religious. Neither of these two interpretations seem very likely as the main thrust, however. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />For me, despite the critical accolades at the time, the story is essentially flawed. I can see what the author was trying to do, and the point he was making, but to me, Robert was just used as a tool to make that point, rather than coming over as an individual in his own right, and I didn’t like that. Yes, we see that the narrator is an awful person, full of prejudice. We grow to see that it is through ignorance, and that this will be a journey of discovery for him. He has his sudden inexplicable insight: the “lightbulb” moment at the end, and realises that he is just as incapacitated by his inability to describe things in words as the blind man is through his lack of sight.<br /><br />But this point would have been much better made, if something was chosen where blind people do regularly excel. For instance, one blind friend of mine has a phenomenal memory. He uses a dictaphone recorder-type device to make notes, but frequently does not refer to it. He has just trained himself to excel at that out of necessity. Sighted people get lazy, and don’t remember things because they don’t have to. Another area where blind people often have the advantage is in technology. Someone blind from birth is likely to have far better computer skills that the average sighted person.<br /><br />There is a spark of hope in that ending - a spark, that is, for the narrator. It is he who learns how to imagine, and to feel. But think about this for a minute. What have sighted people learned about being blind? That the function of blind people is to facilitate something for the sighted? Not cool. For me this reveals an assumption verging on an inner prejudice on the part of the author. So I’m sorry if you find my views extreme. I know that Raymond Carver was making a point about intolerance and ignorance of different sorts - even to the reaction to the name “Beulah”. But I didn’t like that he sacrificed realism, just using a blind person to provide a learning point for the narrator. We are bludgeoned over the head with the narrator’s prejudices; so much so that his way of referring to Robert as <i>“the blind man”</i> left us thinking that this was the main point about him, ie., his function was to facilitate a learning experience in a sighted person, and not to be treated as an individual himself. <br /><br />But perhaps you disagree.<br /><br />It is not a bad story in my view, merely rather one which stops short of investigating the deeper issues here, perhaps because the author did not consider them. Hence, two stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11655562805068875257" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13279825126637259615" style="display:none">"<i>You guys keep talking about the virtues of simplicity—I’ll show you. There’s absolutely nothing to simplicity .....</i>" - Norman Mailer<br /><br />I love Raymond Carver's short stories. But after reading three collections, I feel like anyone could write like him if they practiced. It is a style that is quite easy to imitate. I guess he got there first. So, well done. <br /><br />I liked the following stories in the collection: <br /><br /><b>Feathers</b> - Two couples meet for dinner. There is a peacock at the house of the couple who are the hosts. Carver uses animals to infuse creepiness into his stories. I remember a short story called <b>What's in Alaska?</b> where a cat scares the hell out of one of the characters. <br /><br /><b>Chef's House</b> and <b>Preservation</b> had similar themes. They were about people past their prime. Diseased people just living on and on - dealing with unemployment and alcoholism and relocation. Carver can really make you feel sad without saying alot. <br /><br /><b>Vitamins</b> - great short story about an alcoholic couple. This was probably my favorite. The main character could have been out of a Bukowski short story. <br /><br /><b>Fever</b> - A man finds it hard balancing his job and kids after his wife leaves him.</span>

<div id="review_2154146609" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2154146609" />
    <a title="Trevor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63036279-trevor"><img alt="Trevor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547883502p2/63036279.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2154146609?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trevor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trevor" href="/user/show/63036279-trevor">Trevor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/9468704-cathedral">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63036279-trevor?shelf=short-story-collections">short-story-collections</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63036279-trevor?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63036279-trevor?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63036279-trevor?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2154146609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5083754434781795068">Perfection. Every story in this collection is gold. Raymond Carver is on another level when it comes to writing stories that encapsulate real life and real people, especially the ones who are so often skipped over in literature. There is darkness here, but also hope. Out of the three collections of Carver's I have read, this one had the most redemption. <br/><br/>My favorites:<br/><br/><b>
  <i>A Small, Good Thing</i>
</b> - I don't often cry, while reading or any other time really, but by the end of this story I had shed a few </span>
  <span id="freeText5083754434781795068" style="display:none">Perfection. Every story in this collection is gold. Raymond Carver is on another level when it comes to writing stories that encapsulate real life and real people, especially the ones who are so often skipped over in literature. There is darkness here, but also hope. Out of the three collections of Carver's I have read, this one had the most redemption. <br><br>My favorites:<br><br><b>
  <i>A Small, Good Thing</i>
</b> - I don't often cry, while reading or any other time really, but by the end of this story I had shed a few tears. The raw, emotional charge of this story snuck up on me the way it does to the characters; the humanity in the last page - I get a twinge in the heart strings just thinking about it now. <br><br><b>
  <i>Vitamins</i>
</b> - The main character of this one is kind of a dick, in more ways than one, and there isn't much of a happy ending, but I liked it for the intensity and the perfectly executed theme of stagnation. (Which is pretty prevalent across several of these stories.)<br><br><b>
  <i>Cathedral</i>
</b> - Of course I can't leave this one out. It is considered one of the best short stories of the age for a reason. I will give nothing away except to say that there are several people I know who I think would get a lot out of reading this. Despite the title, it is not religious in nature, but I guess you could consider it spiritual. I don't have the words to do it justice, but even if you don't read the entire collection, just reading this story alone is well worth it.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1508694621i/24244846._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="5083754434781795068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2107664872" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2107664872" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2107664872?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=postmodern-fiction">postmodern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=america-canada">america-canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2107664872" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14792071994855448974">Third collection of Carver's work I have now read, and although I didn't feel it hit the heights of 'What We Talk About When We Talk About Love' he again produces some quite remarkable short stories, with a scrupulously simple prose full of compassion and honesty, all with a keen eye set only on describing and revealing the world as he sees it. He manages to create situations that are so breathtakingly real, and all done in such a short space of time.<br/><br/> I have been a huge admirer of Richard Yates'</span>
  <span id="freeText14792071994855448974" style="display:none">Third collection of Carver's work I have now read, and although I didn't feel it hit the heights of 'What We Talk About When We Talk About Love' he again produces some quite remarkable short stories, with a scrupulously simple prose full of compassion and honesty, all with a keen eye set only on describing and revealing the world as he sees it. He manages to create situations that are so breathtakingly real, and all done in such a short space of time.<br /><br /> I have been a huge admirer of Richard Yates' shorter fiction, and Carver now joins him as one of my fave short story writers. He captures emotions, actions and mannerisms, and all those little things we take for granted in life and sometimes fail to see with our own eyes. He takes the ordinary, mundane folk of America, and just makes them so bloody readable! A true master! A one of kind.<br /><br />All twelve stories in this collection where really good, without a single dud. But If I had to pick out a third of them they would be - A Small, Good Thing, Vitamins, Where I'm Calling from, and Cathedral.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14792071994855448974" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_137494439" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137494439" />
    <a title="Eric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3170906-eric"><img alt="Eric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1263902845p2/3170906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/137494439?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric" href="/user/show/3170906-eric">Eric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer137494439" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2556568946263329322">This is the collection where Carver shakes off the label of "minimalist".<br/><br/>His previous editor, Gordon Lish, was known for paring Carver's stories down to the bone. But in this collection, free from Lish's pencil, he is able to be more expansive. The stories still concern average shmoes living clumsy lives, but now Carver gives himself the space for more incident. More emotional nuance. Not only that, but he's funnier, and he was fairly funny to begin with.<br/><br/>I read a volume of his poems recently. Li</span>
  <span id="freeText2556568946263329322" style="display:none">This is the collection where Carver shakes off the label of "minimalist".<br /><br />His previous editor, Gordon Lish, was known for paring Carver's stories down to the bone. But in this collection, free from Lish's pencil, he is able to be more expansive. The stories still concern average shmoes living clumsy lives, but now Carver gives himself the space for more incident. More emotional nuance. Not only that, but he's funnier, and he was fairly funny to begin with.<br /><br />I read a volume of his poems recently. Like these stories, those poems were written in the eighties, during Carver's sobriety. Whereas poetry is usually about economy of language, finding that perfect word or phrase, Carver's poems were like listening to a friend who's had a few drinks and is telling you a story. If his audience is laughing, he's not afraid to lay it on. Reiterate certain comic points. Be a little redundant. Rant a bit. Play to the crowd. These stories have a bit of that same flavor.<br /><br />The best example is of this is in the title story. The protagonist is a husband who's annoyed that his wife has invited an old friend, a blind man, over to spend the night. The character initially feels uncomfortable about the man's blindness, and expresses this to the reader by sarcastically riffing on how put out he feels, and how alien and creepy the man's blindness seems to him. But this is only the setup. The payoff is truly remarkable, emotional, and inspiring. I won't give it away.<br /><br />Carver's early work was great, but lay in the shadow of his alcoholism, and for that reason was somewhat morbid. The later Carver seems to be poking fun at the morbidity in his past, all the while embracing life and its small wondrous experiences.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2556568946263329322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="J.P." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/424062-j-p"><img alt="J.P." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529364504p2/424062.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.P." class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.P." href="/user/show/424062-j-p">J.P.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">short story writers, short fiction fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6949136" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15273224793727538892">On Christmas Eve, 1989, I sat in my room as snow fell outside. I was 20 years old. That night, I read this book cover-to-cover. I didn't mean to---Carver's voice and characters just grabbed hold of me and wouldn't let go. It's one of the few books I've ever read in one sitting. <br/><br/>These characters, I found, weren't like 'made-up' people from most other fiction I'd read up to that time. They were my friends, neighbors, coworkers---and to some extent, me. <br/><br/>Upon completing Cathedral, I was certain of t</span>
  <span id="freeText15273224793727538892" style="display:none">On Christmas Eve, 1989, I sat in my room as snow fell outside. I was 20 years old. That night, I read this book cover-to-cover. I didn't mean to---Carver's voice and characters just grabbed hold of me and wouldn't let go. It's one of the few books I've ever read in one sitting. <br /><br />These characters, I found, weren't like 'made-up' people from most other fiction I'd read up to that time. They were my friends, neighbors, coworkers---and to some extent, me. <br /><br />Upon completing Cathedral, I was certain of three things: 1.) I wanted to be a writer; 2.) one could be a writer and be a blue-collar person (like Carver), not some glow-in-the-dark genius lounging in an ivory tower somewhere; 3.) I wanted to read every word Carver wrote. <br /><br />18 years later, I've accomplished #3. I'm still working on #1. But when my inspiration flags, I dip back into Cathedral and it always recharges my writer's batteries.<br /><br />This book is a modern classic. By all means, check it out! Raymond Carver truly was "America's Chekhov". </span>
  <a data-text-id="15273224793727538892" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2753142251" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2753142251" />
    <a title="Ilana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59371159-ilana"><img alt="Ilana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563253235p2/59371159.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2753142251?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ilana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ilana" href="/user/show/59371159-ilana">Ilana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099530336" href="/book/show/6521466-cathedral">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=america">america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=readingslowly-over-time">readingslowly-over-time</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2753142251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1495893984467091831">My first book by Raymond Carver. I admire everything he manages to packs into each story but have always had trouble picking up on symbolism and reading into deeper meanings than what I feel intuitively and have difficulty verbalizing. One recurring theme is the difficulty we have communicating with one another and with understanding ourselves. This is borne out with repeated references to heavy drinkers and alcoholism; how so many people choose this easy escape in a glass to deal with inconveni</span>
  <span id="freeText1495893984467091831" style="display:none">My first book by Raymond Carver. I admire everything he manages to packs into each story but have always had trouble picking up on symbolism and reading into deeper meanings than what I feel intuitively and have difficulty verbalizing. One recurring theme is the difficulty we have communicating with one another and with understanding ourselves. This is borne out with repeated references to heavy drinkers and alcoholism; how so many people choose this easy escape in a glass to deal with inconvenient feelings and situations. <br /><br />Not all the stories in this collection involve booze, but miscommunication is a major theme and they are overall rather bleak and depressing, so I took my time reading them bit by bit, not wanting to be dragged down with despair. The title story Cathedral illustrates this perfectly. The narrators wife announces an old blind friend is coming over for a visit after losing his wife to cancer. She and this friend have had a regular correspondence over many years and exchanged many intimate details about their inner lives and their marriages. As for the narrator, he can barely think of a blind person as a fully realized human being and seems to think their lack of sight means they aren’t able to enjoy life. It is soon revealed that while most people are able to look, few take the time to actually <i>see</i>. <br /><br />With his simple pared down language, Carver invites us to look beyond the surface and to find true meaning. I think that’s what he’s doing in any case. I’ll need to read more articles analyzing his work maybe. But then again, sometimes I think it’s okay to just intuit things without seeking to understand them logically either. Our inner eye sees so much more than we allow for after all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1495893984467091831" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4084359812017390927">A free online link to the short story <i>Cathedral</i> is found here: httHow do people best communicateps://<a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.thelondonmagazine.org/cathedral-by-raymond-carver/'>www.thelondonmagazine.org/cathedral-b...</a><br/><br/>Review of the one story <i>Cathedral</i>:<br/><br/>How do people best communicate?   <span id="freeText4084359812017390927" style="display:none">A free online link to the short story <i>Cathedral</i> is found here: httHow do people best communicateps://<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.thelondonmagazine.org/cathedral-by-raymond-carver/">www.thelondonmagazine.org/cathedral-b...</a><br /><br />Review of the one story <i>Cathedral</i>:<br /><br />How do people best communicate? By touch? <br /><br />Communication is the means by which a thought, an idea, a message passes from one individual to another. Words are what first come to mind, but visually we can read another’s disposition and through one person’s touch of another communication is passed too.<br /><br />A very good short story. The author has observed how individuals behave. His words then draw pin-perfectly what he has seen, and we, reading his words, understand without need for explanation. I like the lack of explanation. I like that explanation is not necessary.<br /><br />Communication through observation, through the simplest of words and as the story shows, through touch.</span>

<div id="review_203665781" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/203665781" />
    <a title="Gretchen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5847541-gretchen"><img alt="Gretchen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573842089p2/5847541.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/203665781?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gretchen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gretchen" href="/user/show/5847541-gretchen">Gretchen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5847541-gretchen?shelf=adult-fiction">adult-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer203665781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3806900575694114307">Cathedral is a collection of short stories by Raymond Carver. Each story explores everyday moments in humanity, as well as common issues like relationships and alcoholism. Carver’s writing style is minimalist, so there are no moments of long description of setting or characters. Carver focuses on plot and themes, and each story ends with a slight cliffhanger, leaving it up to the reader to decide what happens.<br/><br/>I read this book for my Contemporary American Literature class, and I loved it. All the</span>
  <span id="freeText3806900575694114307" style="display:none">Cathedral is a collection of short stories by Raymond Carver. Each story explores everyday moments in humanity, as well as common issues like relationships and alcoholism. Carver’s writing style is minimalist, so there are no moments of long description of setting or characters. Carver focuses on plot and themes, and each story ends with a slight cliffhanger, leaving it up to the reader to decide what happens.<br /><br />I read this book for my Contemporary American Literature class, and I loved it. All the stories are very interesting, and they invoke lots of feelings in the reader. Carver’s writing is especially compelling to me because I hate long descriptions. Lord of the Rings was torture with the long descriptions of boring things. Carver focuses on the plots and allows the reader to think only about the important things and not extraneous details.<br /><br />My favorite story was A Good, Small Thing which revolved around a couple dealing with their son being in the hospital. The story was extremely interesting and while depressing, the ending and the title show the reader that there is always something to put your hope into.<br /><br />I definitely recommend this collection of short stories. Each story is fantastic, and it’s a very good read. On another note, I saw that Will Ferrell’s movie Everything Must Go is actually based on a Carver story! Not one that I’ve read, but still, I found it exciting. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3806900575694114307" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1380919209" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1380919209" />
    <a title="Momina Masood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17262906-momina-masood"><img alt="Momina Masood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1423900868p2/17262906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1380919209?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Momina Masood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Momina Masood" href="/user/show/17262906-momina-masood">Momina Masood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17262906-momina-masood?shelf=short-prose">short-prose</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17262906-momina-masood?shelf=american-lit">american-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1380919209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15802318836978825954"><i>Our Favorite Faded Fantasies</i><br/><br/>Carver winds and unwinds the silken threads which hold us together, our essential vulnerability as human beings, our failures, our disappointments. What I have loved about this set of stories is their tenderness and silence. Men and women are explored in a discreet, subtle writing style that is very effective. It implies and leaves much to the imagination but in a way that is not cerebral at all; rather the things Carver leaves unsaid are those we understand to the bo</span>
  <span id="freeText15802318836978825954" style="display:none"><i>Our Favorite Faded Fantasies</i><br><br>Carver winds and unwinds the silken threads which hold us together, our essential vulnerability as human beings, our failures, our disappointments. What I have loved about this set of stories is their tenderness and silence. Men and women are explored in a discreet, subtle writing style that is very effective. It implies and leaves much to the imagination but in a way that is not cerebral at all; rather the things Carver leaves unsaid are those we understand to the bone, a kind of intersubjective, essential knowledge of what it is to be human, something we all share, and something we need not approximate in words. These stories are delicately written, and take for their theme the human subject at his most wishful and perhaps, most heroic. For we are our most courageous when we aspire towards that something elusive, that something that would fix everything if only we could figure what it exactly is, and how to achieve it. But with every dream dreamt comes the point where we wake up, miserably, cut and rendered apart.<br><br><i>“Dreams, you know, are what you wake up from.”</i> -- "The Bridle" <br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1442051810i/16187173._SX540_.png" width="700" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>We swim through the banalities that fill our lives, alone, or sometimes with a kindred spirit we lose during the way—all of us, hoping, seeking for something better. But getting there, finally, out of breath, there is little to look forward to. Up on the wall planted in the middle of an ocean—this is what achieving your dreams look like. The illusion of happiness thins—the grass proves to be the same pale green here, too. But one can see the sunset. One can hold onto the strangers who have made it up with us, all chasing their own idea of happiness. One sits in the light of the setting sun and speaks. We are redeemed by our losses, by our collective disillusionment, we are redeemed in the light of the sun. This is what this book is about. This picture which serves as the cover art for Damien Rice’s recent “My Favorite Faded Fantasy” is what this book is about. <br><br>It’s “A Small, Good Thing” after all, to sit holding hands with a stranger, nibbling on hot rolls and sipping from a warm coffee mug, and sharing grief. It is delicate as peacock “Feathers” to want something more fulfilling, the laughter of a child maybe, and to know that what we seek is unattainable, but the dream is all. But there is pain and distance and loneliness as well. I am trapped in the “Compartment” of my mind which haunts me with memories of an unpleasant past, and no matter how much we try, at the end of the day, it’s better to disappear into oblivion, than to make peace with all that has happened to one. It’s a lovely thing about this book—some walk away, while some reunite, some find solace in the company of others, while some are destined to be alone. But they are all dreamers—all fated to the miserable task of waking up, of losing the source of meaning in their lives, only to be redeemed by the warmth of strangers, of a listening ear. The last and titular story is a worthy climax for this collection. Up on the wall, tired and disillusioned, when the eyes get accustom to “see” the miracles altitude offers, one can trace the very outline of God in the palm of one’s hand. <br><br>We do wonder, all of us, “what it all could be” if…. and so on. But right here, where we are, where time and love has placed us, there is warmth yet. There is beauty and warmth yet, and enough to get by.<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="15802318836978825954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

His writing translates human behavior and ordinary experiences into something warm, comforting and profound. His perspective on human behavior seems spiritual, and he uses words like a poultice that heals a wound.<br/><br/>More to come...<br/>The final story, from which the book gets its title, is a great one.   <span id="freeText14675631926709251935" style="display:none">I just now finished the story "A Small, Good Thing," and I want to share something. Raymond Carver is amazing. His writing translates human behavior and ordinary experiences into something warm, comforting and profound. His perspective on human behavior seems spiritual, and he uses words like a poultice that heals a wound.<br /><br />More to come...<br />The final story, from which the book gets its title, is a great one. Don't let yourself be put off by the main character being an ass. The story has a positive message. I won't try to articulate the message here, but most readers will find that the story has something important to say. <br /><br />Out of 12 stories, only a few really spoke to me, so I am giving the book a 4. It was a good experience, and I plan to read all of Carvers fiction to find my favorites.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2589858062" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16885402511895452418">I believe this short-story is one that would be open to many interpretations, so mine might or might not have any semblance to yours. I thought it was about prejudices, preconceived ideas, and walking a mile in another man's shoes. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UVTbET7mu5NtP0AB4ppP2pBmjR8PaGBagGt/9olvF70kn34inQc19vCbC1m8hNobAVL2ySKUiQ4LcAEtXMUiRg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('H96HXUdZdijNyxLFVYEMUWk1gnTEb2VAlh2fLfYm9v5qFSJu5Lj4TVBvWZ0Ln5mQ+AH5oumTjBQdBuH2I4zDBQ==')})
  }

It is a<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10347538604071177593\" style=\"display:none\">A Good Man Is Hard to Find is Flannery O\'Connor\'s most famous and most discussed story. O\'Connor herself singled it out by making it the title piece of her first collection and the story she most often chose for readings or talks to students. It is an unforgettable tale, both riveting and comic, of the confrontation of a family with violence and sudden death. More than anything else O\'Connor ever wrote, this story mixes the comedy, violence, and religious concerns that characterize her fiction.\n\nThis casebook for the story includes an introduction by the editor, a chronology of the author\'s life, the authoritative text of the story itself, comments and letters by O\'Connor about the story, critical essays, and a bibliography. The critical essays span more than twenty years of commentary and suggest several approaches to the story--formalistic, thematic, deconstructionist-- all within the grasp of the undergraduate, while the introduction also points interested students toward still other resources. Useful for both beginning and advanced students, this casebook provides an in-depth introduction to one of America\'s most gifted modern writers.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"10347538604071177593\"             e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('bookCover_34335877').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7363.Raymond_Carver">About Raymond Carver</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7363.Raymond_Carver"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1525695952p3/7363.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7363.Raymond_Carver">Raymond Carver
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,741
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1909823939756877237">Carver was born into a poverty-stricken family at the tail-end of the Depression. He married at 19, started a series of menial jobs and his own career of 'full-time drinking as a serious pursuit', a career that would eventually kill him. Constantly struggling to support his wife and family, Carver enrolled in a writing programme under author John Gardner in 1958. He saw this opportunity as a turni</span>
  <span id="freeText1909823939756877237" style="display:none">Carver was born into a poverty-stricken family at the tail-end of the Depression. He married at 19, started a series of menial jobs and his own career of 'full-time drinking as a serious pursuit', a career that would eventually kill him. Constantly struggling to support his wife and family, Carver enrolled in a writing programme under author John Gardner in 1958. He saw this opportunity as a turning point.<br /> <br />Rejecting the more experimental fiction of the 60s and 70s, he pioneered a precisionist realism reinventing the American short story during the eighties, heading the line of so-called 'dirty realists' or 'K-mart realists'. Set in trailer parks and shopping malls, they are stories of banal lives that turn on a seemingly insignificant detail. Carver writes with meticulous economy, suddenly bringing a life into focus in a similar way to the paintings of Edward Hopper. As well as being a master of the short story, he was an accomplished poet publishing several highly acclaimed volumes.<br /> <br />After the 'line of demarcation' in Carver's life - 2 June 1977, the day he stopped drinking - his stories become increasingly more redemptive and expansive. Alcohol had eventually shattered his health, his work and his family - his first marriage effectively ending in 1978. He finally married his long-term parter Tess Gallagher (they met ten years earlier at a writers' conference in Dallas) in Reno, Nevada, less than two months before he eventually lost his fight with cancer.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1909823939756877237" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
